For the origins of conscience in legal pleading, I am indebted to C. A. Pierce, [3mConscience in the New Testament [0m(London: SCM 
Press, 1955). This notably perceptive study abounds in fresh insights and is especially to be recommended. 

Valuable observations on Christian traditions of conscience and the predicaments of conscience today are to be found in Paul 
Tillich, the โTransmoral Conscienceโ, in [3mMorality and Beyond [0m(New York: Harper Torchbooks, 1966), pp. 65โ81. 

For a fine discussion of conscience in Lutherโs early theology, see Michael G. Baylor, [3mAction and Person: Conscience in Late 
Scholasticism and the Young Luther [0m(Leiden: Brill, 1977). Some of the possible extremities of Protestant conviction are discussed 
by Steven E. Ozment, [3mMysticism and Dissent: Religious Ideology and Social Protest in the Sixteenth Century [0m(New Haven and London: 
Yale University Press, 1973). 

Many of the writings considered in my second chapter, together with other subjects of importance, are treated with impressive 
depth in Edward G. Andrew, [3mConscience and Its Critics: Protestant Conscience, Enlightenment Reason, and Modern Subjectivity [0m
(Toronto and London: University of Toronto Press, 2001). Highly recommended. 

Especially pertinent to Kant, and also richly engaged with other themes treated in this book, is Thomas E. Hill, โFour Conceptions 
of Conscienceโ, [3mHuman Welfare and Moral Worth: Kantian Perspectives [0m(Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2002). 

Additional perspectives on issues treated here are to be found in Charles Taylor, [3mSources of the Self [0m(Cambridge: Cambridge 
University Press, 1989) and Paul Ricoeur, [3mOneself as Another [0m(Chicago and London: University of Chicago Press, 1992).
